	•	Unique constraints & duplicates: Verifies that primary keys (like (indicator_id, date) for a time series) remain unique. If any duplicate key is attempted, the database will reject it (and the ETL will catch this as a no-op or error).
	•	Referential integrity: Ensures that references (like a foreign key linking a data record to a source ID or sector ID) remain valid. The Master Source Registry itself is stored in the database, so each data point carries a source reference that must match an entry in the registry.
	•	Content hash validation: For document text extraction, after OCR or text parsing, a hash of the text can be compared to ensure no corruption in transit. Similarly, numeric dataset files may have known checksums (especially if provided by source) that YETO verifies before processing.
	•	Outlier detection: (As an added integrity step) After data is in, an automated QA script scans for extreme outliers or sudden changes that might indicate an ingestion error (for example, a GDP series suddenly dropping to zero could mean a parsing issue). While not all outliers are errors, the system raises warnings for review if something deviates beyond thresholds.

All integrity checks run nightly as part of a Continuous Validation process. Any failure in these checks (duplicate entries, missing provenance, schema mismatch, etc.) results in an automatic alert and the creation of an issue in the system (e.g., an admin “to-do” or internal bug ticket). This ensures data quality issues are caught promptly, and ingestion pipelines maintain trust in the data.

Additionally, each pipeline run produces a log entry and is marked with a status: Success, Partial, or Failed. Partial successes (e.g., one file out of two failed) trigger safe rollback or isolation of the failed portion. Administrators can review these logs in an “Ingestion Monitor” UI that shows each job’s last run time and result.

Manual Ingestion & OCR Protocol

Not all sources have an API or structured feed. Manual ingestion processes are integrated for those that require human intervention:
	•	Scheduled reminders: For sources expected periodically (like annual budgets or monthly PDF bulletins), the system has scheduled tasks that check for new content availability. This could be as simple as an EventBridge rule that, on schedule, pings a known URL for an update or sends a notification to an admin that “Source X data is due”. If the system cannot auto-fetch the content (due to it being a PDF behind a website or a physical report), it creates a task in an Admin Queue.
	•	Admin Queue & Flags: The admin interface lists manual sources that need attention, flagged by due date or by dynamic discovery (e.g., “Budget 2022 not ingested yet – awaiting file”). Admins or data managers can then obtain the file and upload it into YETO’s intake interface.
	•	Upload & parsing: When an admin uploads a document (PDF/Excel), it triggers the ingestion pipeline for that source. For tabular data in Excel, a specialized parser script (per the source’s known format) runs to extract data. For PDFs that are tabular, if OCR is needed (scanned docs), the system uses an OCR engine (like Tesseract or AWS Textract) to get text, then applies parsing rules to find tables or key numbers.
	•	Two-step verification: Because manual ingestion can be error-prone, YETO implements a two-step verification for critical manual data. After data is extracted, it is presented in the admin UI for review. For example, an extracted budget table is shown to an analyst who confirms that the figures align with the PDF before finalizing load. This avoids garbage-in from OCR mistakes.
	•	Provenance capture: The manual ingestion tool requires the admin to tag the source document and metadata (e.g., “Budget 2022, published by MoF on X date”) so that this context is saved. The system attaches this metadata to every data point ingested from the document (for traceability when users query it).
	•	Scheduling & tagging: If a manual source is not strictly periodic, the Discovery Engine (discussed later) helps find when a new document might be available. For example, YETO might detect a press release about a new Economic Plan PDF – it will then flag the Source Registry entry “Economic Plan” as having potential new input, prompting admin to fetch it.
	•	Scanned documents: In cases like historical reports or leaked documents that are image scans, the pipeline’s OCR kicks in. The text output is stored and linked with the document. If translation is needed (for Arabic documents, see translation pipeline below), that also occurs. The OCR quality is validated by checking if key expected keywords are present (e.g., for a budget, the presence of “Total Revenue” in Arabic). If OCR confidence is low, the system marks the document for manual transcription to ensure important figures aren’t misread.
	•	Manual data pipeline logging: Each manual ingestion action by an admin is logged (user, time, what was ingested) to maintain an audit trail. This is important for trust and for repeating the process if needed (e.g., if a better-quality document becomes available later, one can re-run ingestion).

In summary, manual pipelines are treated with equal rigor as automated ones: scheduled reminders, structured admin workflow for input, and thorough logging. The platform design assumes that some data will always need this path (e.g., government documents, partner spreadsheets), so it builds in the capacity to handle those systematically rather than ad hoc. Such sources are also clearly flagged in the Source Registry (with Access Method “manual” and Ingestion Method “manual pipeline”) so that everyone knows those require human in-the-loop.

Document Translation & Bilingual Data Pipeline

YETO is a bilingual platform (Arabic-English), so the ingestion pipeline includes an integrated machine translation step for all textual content:
	•	Extraction: When a new document is ingested (whether via automation or manual upload), its original text is extracted (if not already in text form, OCR precedes this). The pipeline identifies the source language (e.g., via metadata or language detection on text).
	•	Translation: If the document (or data field) is in Arabic, it is machine-translated to English; if it’s in English, it’s translated to Arabic. This uses a translation engine – likely an AWS Translate API or a custom trained model – configured for Yemeni Arabic terminology. The translation engine is constrained by a controlled glossary: YETO maintains a bilingual glossary of key terms (especially economic and institutional terms) to ensure consistent translation (e.g., “Central Bank” <-> “البنك المركزي”, specific ministry names, etc.). The translation system enforces these – meaning it will prefer glossary terms and flag any deviations.
	•	Storing translations: The system stores both versions of the text in the vector store and document repository. For each document, the database record includes fields like text_en and text_ar (or references to where those are stored), along with a source_language field. The translation metadata (which model used, version, date, and a confidence score) is recorded in the provenance ledger for the translated text. This way, any piece of text the AI cites has attribution not only to the original document but also notes that it’s a translation (if applicable) from a certain engine, in case quality issues arise.
	•	Parallel data indexing: Because we have both languages, the search index is built to be bilingual. The OpenSearch full-text index has both Arabic and English content indexed so that a user searching in Arabic finds relevant documents that were originally English (via the translated content) and vice versa. Similarly, embeddings are generated for both language versions, or a single multilingual embedding model is used, so that semantic search works across languages.
	•	Translation verification: A QA process is in place for translations. The pipeline automatically compares the length and some keywords of the translation to the original to catch gross errors (for example, if a table of numbers failed to translate properly or got garbled). If confidence (as reported by the model or measured by consistency checks) is below a threshold, the document is flagged for human review in the admin interface. The bilingual glossary adherence is also scored – if the translation engine did not use a preferred glossary term, that instance is flagged for a linguist to potentially update the glossary or correct the text.
	•	Dynamic translation for data values: In addition to documents, any data field names and descriptions in the platform are stored bilingually. For example, indicator names from the World Bank come in English – YETO’s ingestion will look up if an official Arabic name exists (some sources provide them; if not, the system translates the indicator name and saves it). This ensures that, on the Arabic UI, indicator names, units, and metadata appear in Arabic. The translation pipeline component handles these small text pieces as well, using the glossary (e.g., “GDP (current US$)” is translated to “الناتج المحلي الإجمالي (بالسعر الجاري بالدولار)”).
	•	Storage of original vs. translation: The system never discards the original text. The PostgreSQL database or S3 store keeps original-language content. The translated text is stored alongside it. Pages in the platform can toggle between original and translated content (important for transparency, especially if an official document is ingested – users can switch to see the original PDF or text). The provenance ledger for any answer the AI gives will indicate if it’s quoting a translated text.
	•	Real-time translation for user queries: As an extension, if a user asks a question in Arabic but the relevant data or document is only in English in the database, the retrieval system will fetch the English answer and then use the same translation engine on-the-fly to present it in Arabic (while still providing source citation to the original English source). Similarly, Arabic source data can be delivered to an English query with translation. This dynamic translation leverages the stored translations when available (for pre-ingested docs) or performs it live if needed.

By incorporating translation into ingestion, YETO ensures that language is not a barrier to any content: every piece of information is available in both English and Arabic. It also means the AI assistant can draw on the full corpus regardless of language, and always respond in the user’s language of choice with proper terminology. This bilingual pipeline is crucial in Yemen’s context, where some data sources and reports are only in Arabic and others only in English – YETO bridges that gap. All translations are done in a reproducible way (with model/version logged) to maintain consistency and allow updates if the translation quality improves with a new model version (documents can be re-translated in batch if needed, using the stored originals).

3. Source–Sector–Module Map

Each data source in YETO is mapped to the relevant economic sectors it informs, the platform modules/pages that utilize its data, its temporal coverage, and the primary stakeholder groups interested in that source. The table below provides a matrix of these relationships, including the sector names in English and Arabic:

Source	Sector (English & Arabic)	Platform Modules	Time Coverage	Key User Roles
World Bank Open Data (Indicators)	Macroeconomic & Trade Indicators (مؤشرات الاقتصاد الكلي والتجارة)	Macro Trends, Governance, Scenario Simulator, Data Explorer API, Methodology	1960–present	Policymakers, Donors/IFIs, Researchers

ACLED (Armed Conflict Location & Event Data)	Conflict Events (أحداث الصراع)	Interactive Timeline, Maps, AI Evidence base, Sanctions/Compliance	2015–present	Donors/UN (stabilization), Analysts/Researchers, Media

Ministry of Finance/Planning/CSO (Yemen)	Public Finance & Economic Planning (المالية العامة والتخطيط الاقتصادي)	Public Finance Dashboard, Debt Tracker, Economic Plans, Research Library	2010–present	Policymakers, Donors/IFIs, Researchers/Analysts

ACAPS Yemen Analysis (YETI)	Research/Think-Tank (الأبحاث / مراكز الفكر)	Scenario Simulator, Research Hub, AI Assistant	2010–present	Donors & UN planners, Analysts, Media
Rethinking Yemen’s Economy (RYE)	Research/Think-Tank (الأبحاث / مراكز الفكر)	Stakeholder pages, Glossary, Research Hub	2010–present	Donors, Policymakers, Think tanks/Researchers
UN & National Sanctions Lists (UN, OFAC, EU, UK)	Sanctions & Compliance (العقوبات والامتثال)	Sanctions Dashboard, Stakeholder Profiles, AI Compliance Q&A, Timeline	2010–present	Banks (Compliance), Regulators, NGOs/Donors

How to read this matrix: Each source often spans multiple sectors and modules. For example, IMF data is used in Macroeconomic analysis, Financial Sector insights, and Public Finance comparisons, feeding into dashboards like Macro Trends, Financial Sector, etc. Time coverage indicates roughly the period for which data is available (not necessarily continuously). Key user roles denote which audiences benefit from or rely on that source’s information on the platform – e.g., Policymakers (government officials) and Donors frequently use macro and aid data, Banks and regulators watch financial and compliance data, Humanitarian agencies monitor aid flows, Researchers and think tanks use a broad range of data for analysis, and the general public benefits via curated indicators in the platform’s Arabic interface.

This mapping ensures that for every YETO module or dashboard, the underlying data sources are identified, and conversely, every source’s utility in the platform is clear. It also helps in identifying coverage gaps – if a sector or module had no sources, that gap would stand out here (and trigger the gap management process).

4. Maher AI Coaching for Ingestion Traceability

Maher AI is the intelligent “coach” ensuring that the YETO platform’s AI assistants remain grounded in the ingested evidence and maintain complete traceability for every answer. Both the super-admin agent (Maher) and the user-facing AI assistants are designed to use the provenance ledger and latest data as their sole knowledge base. Key principles and features include:
	•	Evidence-Grounded Answers: When a user asks a question, the AI does not rely on any hardcoded or potentially outdated knowledge – it dynamically retrieves information from YETO’s updated database and document store. For instance, if asked, “What is Yemen’s inflation rate this year?”, the assistant will query the inflation time series in the database (populated by the latest CSO or WB data) and get the most recent value. All intermediate steps (which query run, which data fetched) are transparent to the Maher AI layer for logging. This ensures the answer reflects only the latest ingested data, not the AI model’s training data or stale info.
	•	Automated Citation of Sources: The assistant’s answer generation pipeline includes a step that attaches source citations drawn from the provenance ledger. Every figure, fact, or quote presented is accompanied by a reference to its source (e.g., “Central Bank of Yemen (Aden) bulletin, Jan 2023” or “World Bank 2021 data, CC-BY-4.0 licensed”). In practice, the user interface may show these as footnotes or inline reference tags that can be clicked to view source details. The AI is “coached” by Maher to always include these – it has been given a hard rule that “every number shown must have a source pack”. A “source pack” is essentially the bundle of source name, date, and any licensing info relevant. For example, if the AI says “GDP grew 2% in 2022,” it will append something like a footnote linking to “【World Bank – World Development Indicators, updated 2023, CC-BY】”. This is done automatically by looking up the provenance ledger entry for the GDP 2022 data point it pulled.
	•	Real-Time Contradiction Checking: When retrieving data, Maher AI cross-checks multiple sources if they exist for the query. If the user’s question is such that two sources might have answers (e.g., “What is Yemen’s GDP in 2019 according to international sources?” and both World Bank and IMF have that figure), the AI retrieves both. If they agree (or one clearly designated as primary), fine – but if they conflict (say WB says $24 billion, IMF says $26 billion for 2019 GDP), the system triggers a contradiction flag. Maher AI has a “Disagreement Mode” which can be activated: in this mode, instead of giving a single figure, the assistant will explicitly note the discrepancy. For instance, it might answer: “The GDP for 2019 is estimated around $25 billion, but sources differ (World Bank: $24.7B, IMF: $26.1B)【WB 2020†; IMF 2020†】. There is a ~6% variance due to different methodologies.” This way, the end-user is informed of conflicting data rather than the AI silently choosing one. The contradiction is also logged in the system’s knowledge base – admins can see a list of such data points where sources don’t align, which helps identify areas needing reconciliation or user explanation pages.
	•	Disagreement Mode for Users: Users can also explicitly invoke “Show disagreements” (e.g., a toggle in the UI) which tells the AI to surface any divergent figures in its answer. Maher AI ensures that when this mode is on, the assistant will present a comparative view: e.g., “According to the Central Bank in Aden, inflation is X%, but the Sana’a authorities have not reported recent figures; the World Bank’s modelled estimate is Y%【WB 2022†】. These differences are flagged for your awareness.” This feature builds trust, as it exposes the complexity of Yemen’s data environment rather than giving a false illusion of certainty.
	•	Source Preference & Role Context: The AI is configured with context on which sources are generally more authoritative for which sectors. For example, for macroeconomic questions, Maher AI might prioritize World Bank or IMF data (and still mention local sources if available). For humanitarian funding questions, OCHA FTS is primary. This preference is implemented via the provenance metadata (each dataset has a reliability score and perhaps a primary tag). The assistant uses that to decide which citation to put first or emphasize. However, it will never completely ignore a relevant source unless directed by reliability rules. The Maher AI coaching includes a rule like “if multiple sources, cite the one with highest reliability score first, but list others if significantly different.”
	•	License Awareness: All data and documents have license info recorded (open, restricted, etc.). The AI assistant is aware of this and will respect license constraints. For open data, it freely cites exact figures. If a dataset were restricted (say ACLED’s detailed data), the assistant knows not to reveal the raw data unless allowed. It might instead give a qualitative answer or an aggregate and say “(source data is restricted, exact details limited)”. Maher AI ensures that any output complies with the licensing – for instance, if using Creative Commons data that requires attribution, the citation given fulfills that requirement. This prevents any inadvertent misuse of proprietary data and is transparently communicated.
	•	Provenance in AI Reasoning: Under the hood, when the AI assistant formulates an answer, it operates by retrieving relevant pieces of data/documents and then generating text. Maher AI injects the provenance context into this chain-of-thought. That means the assistant “knows” along with each fact where it came from and will include that in the final answer. The AI’s language model might internally annotate facts like [GDP=24.7B, source=WB WDI 2021] during generation to make sure it attaches it. This coaching is done via prompt engineering and the system design where the assistant template always includes a citation placeholder for factual answers.
	•	Ensuring No Data Without Source: As a safeguard, after an answer is generated, a post-processing check runs (this is effectively Maher AI acting as a validator). It scans the answer text for any numeric values or specific claims and verifies each has an attached citation reference. If it finds an uncited number, it will reject the answer output and ask the assistant to revise including the missing citation or remove the unsourced fact. This enforces the “every number has a source pack” rule strictly. For example, if the AI said “unemployment is high at 35%” without citing, Maher will intercept and say (internally) “citation needed for 35% – provide source or remove.” The user only sees the final properly sourced version.
	•	Audit Trail & Logs: Every AI answer delivered to users is logged with its provenance – essentially an “answer pack” that Maher AI stores: the final answer text, the sources cited (with IDs pointing to the ledger), and the underlying data retrieved. This means an administrator could later click on an answer and see exactly which data points were used. It’s part of YETO’s evidence system and helps debug or clarify responses if needed.
	•	Contradiction Alerts to Admins: When the AI flags a contradiction (like the IMF vs WB GDP example), besides informing the user, it also raises a notification in the admin dashboard. This might list “Indicator GDP 2019: IMF = 26.1, WB = 24.7 (discrepancy 6%).” The platform’s curators can then decide if they want to add a footnote in the data catalog about this, or investigate the cause (often differences in methodology). Over time, this contributes to a knowledge base of known discrepancies which the assistant can also use to provide richer answers (e.g., “There is no single agreed figure for X; sources range from A to B”).
	•	AI Assistant Roles and Data Scope: There are different AI agents in YETO (for example, a public-facing Q&A assistant, vs an admin-only data explorer, vs Maher AI itself). Maher AI ensures all these agents operate on the same single source of truth. The admin agent might have broader access (e.g., it can see raw data and internal logs). For instance, if an admin asks Maher AI “when was the last time source X was updated?” Maher can answer from the ingestion logs. The user-facing assistant won’t expose those internals but can say “data last updated 2 weeks ago per source”. All agents share the provenance-centric approach; none have a separate unsupervised knowledge.

In essence, Maher AI’s coaching establishes a culture of “trust but verify” within the AI’s functioning – every answer is only as good as the latest data in the system, and it’s always accompanied by the proof (citations). This traceability not only builds user trust but also allows YETO to meet high standards of transparency and accuracy in an environment often plagued by misinformation or data disputes.

5. Gap Management & Dynamic Discovery

YETO’s architecture includes a proactive Gap Management system and a Dynamic Discovery engine to ensure no data blind spots persist without action. This approach acknowledges that data in Yemen’s context can be missing, delayed, or newly emerging, and the platform must adapt by identifying gaps and finding new sources continuously. Key mechanisms:
	•	Automated Data Gap Detection: Each source in the Master Registry has an expected update frequency or “lag tolerance” (e.g., monthly data might be expected within 40 days of month’s end, annual data perhaps within 1 year of previous). The ingestion monitor automatically checks if new data has arrived within the expected interval. If a source hasn’t provided an update beyond that buffer, the system flags a gap. For example, if the Central Bank’s weekly auction data hasn’t been updated in two weeks, or the IMF quarterly data is two quarters overdue, the system will mark these as “stale”. Similarly, if a new platform module or indicator is supposed to have data but none is present, that’s a gap. When a gap is identified, the system immediately creates a Data Gap Ticket in an internal tracker. The ticket contains details like: “Missing update: Consumer Price Index – no data after Dec 2021. Expected monthly updates, lag now 6 months. Why it matters: CPI is needed for inflation dashboard. Probable source: CSO or alternative needed. Action: find recent CPI or proxy (WFP price index). Priority: High.” This structured info helps admins prioritize and address the gap.
	•	“Not Available Yet” User Messaging: While the backend logs a gap ticket, the frontend also reflects the gap openly. Wherever a chart or figure is supposed to appear and data is missing, YETO displays a “Not available” message (with perhaps a tooltip “Data not updated – we’re working on sourcing it”). Maher AI is trained to answer queries on that topic by saying data is not currently available rather than guessing. This transparency avoids any temptation to fill gaps with unsourced info. The creation of a gap ticket is also triggered at the UI level when a user query cannot be answered due to missing data – the assistant will apologize that data isn’t available and (behind the scenes) log a gap for that metric, alerting the team that users are interested in something we lack.
	•	Gap Ticket Workflow: All gap tickets flow into an Admin “Gap Dashboard”. Each ticket includes: what is missing, why it matters (which modules/indicators are affected), recommended sources or next steps, expected update frequency, and priority level. For instance, “Gap #27: No updated poverty rate after 2014. Sources: Household budget survey data needed – maybe World Bank model or UNDP estimate. Action: engage CSO or use modeled estimate.” Admins can then assign these tickets for follow-up. When resolved (data found or ingested), the ticket is closed and linked to the update (e.g., “updated via WB estimate on 2023-05-01”).
	•	Manual Ingestion Fallbacks: If an automated pipeline fails or a source stops providing data via normal means, YETO enables manual fallback. For example, if an API is down for an extended period, the gap ticket will suggest a manual workaround: “Attempt manual download from source website or use last resort alternate.” The ingestion framework is flexible so that an admin can manually upload a file to backfill data for that source, as mentioned in the manual ingestion protocol. The gap ticket acts as a trigger for such fallback. Additionally, if a data source announces it will stop publishing (scenario: “Central Bank will no longer issue money supply stats”), the team can preemptively plan a fallback (like use IMF data or estimate based on proxy) and record that plan in the registry (in the Fallbacks field). These fallbacks are encoded as rules: e.g., “if API returns 404 for 3 days, switch to scraping alternate page” or “if no official inflation, compute proxy from WFP prices”. The system can execute simple fallback logic automatically, and more complex ones via manual intervention.
	•	Emergent Data Gap – New Sector or Module: YETO is designed to expand. When a new thematic module is added (say, an “Education Dashboard”), initially there may be no sources covering it. The gap engine recognizes “Module Education has 0 data sources” as a gap. It will generate a Data Gap Ticket for source discovery: “No data for Education sector module. Potential sources: UNESCO, MoE exam stats, UNICEF reports. Action: search and integrate relevant data.” This ensures that new content areas don’t go live without at least a plan for data population. The platform might label such a module “coming soon” until sources are found, rather than showing empty charts.
	•	Continuous Source Discovery Engine: YETO employs a Dynamic Discovery process to find new or updated data sources on a weekly cycle. Using AWS EventBridge, a weekly discovery job is scheduled (for example, every Sunday). This job performs automated searches and checks across various channels:
	•	ReliefWeb & Humanitarian Data Exchange (HDX): The system queries APIs and RSS feeds for any new Yemen-related datasets or reports. For instance, it might search ReliefWeb for “Yemen Economy” to catch new assessments, or check HDX for new datasets tagged Yemen (like new surveys or NGO data releases).
	•	IATI & FTS changes: While YETO already ingests these, the discovery engine looks for new organizations or data categories not previously in the registry (e.g., a new donor starts reporting Yemen aid on IATI – the system finds this and flags to possibly highlight that source separately).
	•	OpenAlex or academic publications: The discovery job can use OpenAlex (academic index) to find recent research papers on Yemen’s economy. If, say, a university study published new data (like a survey of food security), the engine notes it as a potential source.
	•	Local news and government sites: Web scraping routines lightly monitor official news portals or ministry websites for keywords like “statistical release”, “report”, “index”. If the Ministry of Planning suddenly posts a “Socio-Economic Outlook 2023” PDF, YETO will catch the link and queue it for review.
	•	Global data portals: It checks World Bank’s data catalog for new Yemen entries, IMF’s data portal for any new datasets (like IMF launching a new climate risk dataset that includes Yemen, etc.), and other UN agency portals.
	•	Think tanks and NGOs: It looks at sites like ACAPS (we already have), Sana’a Center (RYE we have), World Bank Blogs, or others for new content or data visualizations related to Yemen.
The result of each weekly scan is a list of “Discovered Items” – could be completely new sources or just new datasets from known sources. Each item is not automatically ingested; instead, it goes to a Source Review Queue for admins.
	•	Admin Source Review Queue: This queue lists newly discovered potential sources or datasets with basic info (title, URL, suspected relevance). Admins then vet each:
	•	If it’s a valuable new source (e.g., “UNICEF just published a survey on school enrollment in Yemen”), the admin can promote it: add a record to the Master Source Registry, assign it to relevant sectors (“Education”), and configure an ingestion method (maybe one-time CSV import or periodic if recurring).
	•	If it’s not relevant or a duplicate of something we have, they can dismiss it.
	•	If credentials are needed (e.g., discovered a dataset behind a login), the item is flagged with a credential required tag. The admin can obtain credentials or decide to skip.
	•	The review queue also warns of licensing: if the discovery engine finds something but license is unclear or restrictive, it flags “licensing warning”. For instance, if a private consultancy’s report is found, the admin must check if it can be used. YETO’s policy is to not ingest anything that isn’t clearly allowed, so those may remain in queue until resolved.
	•	Credential & Licensing Management: For sources requiring API keys or logins (like ACLED or some national sources), the Source Registry marks them. The system will raise alerts for any credential issues – e.g., key expiration. These appear in an admin panel showing which keys are active, about to expire, or causing auth failures. Admins (with proper roles) can update credentials in a secure vault. The ingestion pipelines read from this secure store at runtime. If a credential is missing entirely (say we add a new source that needs an API key), that source’s status remains “Pending credentials” and the pipeline will not run until provided – preventing repeated failures. The gap management thus includes not just data absence but also missing credentials as actionable gaps. For licensing, every source entry includes license info (open, restricted, etc.). If a discovered item has an unclear license, YETO will not automatically ingest it; a human must verify. The system can provide a recommended action: “License unclear – attempt to contact publisher or find alternative open source.” Admins can mark in the registry if a source is approved despite restrictions (perhaps via special permission) or if it must be excluded.
	•	Alerts and Notifications: YETO provides notifications for critical gap events. For example, if a daily source has gone 3 cycles with failures, it sends an email/slack to admins: “Alert: No updates from Source X for 3 weeks, please investigate (Gap Ticket #45).” Similarly, if discovery finds a high-priority dataset (like a new Yemen Humanitarian Needs Overview report), it might notify: “New potential source found: 2025 HNO report – awaiting review.” This keeps the team agile in responding to data developments.

In summary, gap management ensures YETO remains up-to-date and honest about what it doesn’t have, while dynamic discovery ensures the platform’s coverage of Yemen data keeps expanding over time. Together, these systems create a feedback loop: identified gaps drive discovery for new sources, and discovered sources fill gaps and potentially open new questions. All of this is done in a structured, trackable way via tickets and queues, so nothing falls through the cracks without someone on the team knowing.

Scheduled Ingestion Calendar: (Cadence and scheduling of automated jobs)

Weekly	IATI updates, ACLED events, CBY auctions	AWS EventBridge weekly cron (e.g., Mon 01:00 UTC)
Monthly	World Bank, Comtrade, UNCTADstat, ILOSTAT, FAOSTAT, WFP prices, Fuel bulletin, Yemen Data Project (airstrikes), Night Lights	AWS EventBridge monthly cron (1st of month)
Quarterly	IMF datasets (IFS), IOM DTM new surveys	AWS EventBridge quarterly cron (e.g., Jan/Apr/Jul/Oct)
Annual	UCDP conflict data, UNHCR stats, Yemen budget, SFD report	AWS EventBridge annual cron (start of year)
Ad-hoc / As available	CBY reports (circulars), MoF/Planning docs, ACAPS/RYE reports, AYB/Chambers data	Event-driven (discovery or manual trigger)

Ingestion Fallback Logic: (Common failure scenarios and mitigation strategies)

Failure Scenario	Fallback Strategy
API source not reachable or returns error	Automatic retry with exponential backoff; if still failing after several attempts, log the error and notify an admin. The pipeline will attempt again on the next scheduled run. After multiple consecutive failures, trigger an alert and create a Data Gap Ticket for investigation.
Data not updated beyond expected lag	Raise an ingestion alert and generate a Data Gap Ticket specifying the missing update. The system continues to display the last available data with a “stale” warning. In the background, flag the source for manual follow-up (e.g., contact provider or check alternative sources). No fabrication of data – the UI will show “Not available” for the new period until data arrives.
API key or credential expired	Pause automated queries for that source to avoid further errors. Send an immediate alert to administrators to update credentials. In the interim, if a partial public dataset is accessible without the key, use that in a limited capacity. Resume normal ingestion once credentials are updated (documented in credentials.yml). A Data Gap Ticket is logged to track the outage and resolution of credentials.
Source file format or schema changed	The ingestion job detects a parsing error or unexpected schema (e.g., new columns). It halts further processing for that source. The system logs a detailed error for developers and raises an alert. The Data Gap Ticket captures the need to update the ETL mapping. The previous data remains intact; the new data is not ingested until the parser is adjusted. The system may attempt a best-effort partial import if feasible, but typically will stop to prevent corrupt data.
Scheduled manual source not published	If a routinely expected manual update (e.g., a monthly bulletin or annual report) is not found by the scheduled date, the system flags it as delayed. An alert is sent to the content team to obtain the data through other channels (reach out to partners or check again later). The source’s entry is marked “Awaiting update” and a Data Gap Ticket is opened. The pipeline will keep checking on subsequent schedule cycles, and once the document is obtained, it will be ingested.
Restricted/licensed dataset unavailable	If a source becomes temporarily inaccessible due to licensing (e.g., required payment or permission), the system will not ingest unauthorized data. It marks the dataset as “restricted” in the platform (so users know data is currently not available due to access issues). A Data Gap Ticket is created outlining legal/acquisition options (such as obtaining a license, or using aggregate data that is permitted). The ingestion pipeline may fall back to ingesting only permissible metadata or summaries if possible. For example, ACLED detailed data might be halted, but overall monthly fatality counts (allowed under fair use) could be stored as a stop-gap.

Maher AI Traceability Logic: (How the AI assistant ensures source-grounded responses)

Query or Situation	AI Assistant Behavior (Traceability)
User asks a factual question (e.g., an economic indicator)	The assistant retrieves the answer from YETO’s live database (not from memory) and composes a response using the latest ingested data. It attaches a citation referencing the exact source (dataset name, date of data, license) from the provenance ledger. The answer is thus directly traceable to, say, “World Bank – GDP 2021” or “Central Bank Aden, Jan 2023 bulletin,” giving users confidence in its accuracy.
Multiple sources have different values for the requested data	The assistant detects the discrepancy and either chooses the most trusted source (per configuration) or informs the user of the differences. It can invoke a “Disagreement Mode” to present both values side-by-side with their sources, highlighting the variance. For example, it might say, “IMF estimates GDP at X, whereas World Bank estimates Y for the same year,” with both sources cited, rather than providing a single potentially misleading figure.
The answer contains statistical figures or quotes from documents	Every such figure or quote is accompanied by a citation. The assistant ensures each number or specific fact is linked to a source entry. A post-processing check (Maher AI’s oversight) scans the draft answer to verify no numeric claim lacks a reference. If any are found, the assistant is prompted to add the missing citation or remove the unsupported number. This guarantees that no data point is presented without its provenance.
Data for a query is missing or out-of-date	The assistant responds transparently that the information is not available in the current data. It might say, “As of the latest data we have, this metric is not reported.” It will not hallucinate an answer. It provides context if possible (e.g., last known value or why it’s missing) and cites the source for that last data point if relevant. Simultaneously, the system’s gap engine logs this inquiry – triggering a Data Gap Ticket if one isn’t already open – so that the missing data can be acquired in the future. The AI thus acts as a sentinel, both informing the user honestly and feeding back the unmet need to the system.

This concludes Part 1 of the YETO implementation, establishing the foundation for a live data operation, evidence-backed user experience, and the Maher AI brain. All specifications above are ready for engineering hand-off, ensuring that data ingestion, management, and AI interaction meet the requirements for transparency, reliability, and adaptability in Yemen’s challenging data environment.


Methodology & Transparency / المنهجية والشفافية

Master Data Source Registry (English)

The YETO platform maintains a comprehensive Master Data Source Registry that catalogues every data source, dataset, and document collection used. This registry ensures each indicator and insight can be traced back to its origin and acquisition method . For each source, the registry records key metadata: source name, publisher/organization, data content, access method (API, CSV, PDF, manual), authentication or permissions needed, update frequency, coverage scope, licensing/terms, reliability rating, and any known caveats . This information helps administrators manage data ingestion and quality control, while providing transparency to users about where data comes from. The registry is dynamic – new sources are continuously discovered and added via an automated Source Discovery Engine (which feeds a review queue for approval)  . Below is an overview of the primary sources in the registry, grouped by category:

Global Economic & Development Data Sources
These are major international databases and organizations providing macro-economic, trade, and development indicators for Yemen (and global comparators):

Source & Data	Access & Requirements	Update Frequency	Notes / Caveats
World Bank Open Data – Country development indicators, global economic data, and research reports (World Bank).	API (JSON, open access; no API key required) 	Continuous (data updated as released; queries available daily) 	Comprehensive official data covering economics, poverty, infrastructure, etc., with global coverage (including Yemen). High reliability source .
World Bank Projects & Operations – Project budgets, locations, results, documents (World Bank).	API (Projects endpoint; requires registration or partnership for full access) 	Quarterly updates (project data refreshed periodically) 	Detailed project-level data on aid and development projects. Some datasets require coordination with World Bank offices for access (not openly documented).
IMF Data (SDMX/IFS) – Macroeconomic and financial statistics (IMF International Financial Statistics, Balance of Payments, etc.).	API via SDMX format (some endpoints open; others require IMF API key) 	Quarterly (most series; some monthly) 	Authoritative global economic data (e.g. GDP, exchange rates, monetary aggregates). High credibility official source; registration may be needed for certain datasets.
UN Comtrade – International trade statistics by country and product (UN Statistics Division).	REST API (public, free up to query limits) 	Monthly (detailed trade data); also annual aggregates 	Extensive trade flow data (imports/exports). Large queries are capped – may require batch downloads. Data is official but subject to reporting lags and omissions.
UNCTADstat – Trade & development indicators (UNCTAD).	Bulk download (CSV/Excel via website; registration needed for some data) 	Annual (varies by dataset) 	Covers FDI, trade in services, commodity prices and more, complementing Comtrade. Free for most datasets; some segments may require credentials .
ILO STAT – Labor market indicators (ILO).	REST API (requires API key registration) 	Annual (with some quarterly sub-series) 	Employment, unemployment, wages, and informality statistics. Official ILO data; may have gaps for Yemen. Requires coordination with ILO for any special data or context .
FAOSTAT – Agriculture and food security data (FAO).	Bulk data download (CSV from FAOSTAT platform; open access) 	Annual (production yearbooks, etc.) 	Crops, livestock, food supply and prices. Global datasets with Yemen extracts. High-quality data; some indicators updated less frequently.
WHO/UNICEF Health & Nutrition – Health outcomes and nutrition surveys (WHO Global Health Observatory, UNICEF MICS).	APIs and downloads (SDMX or custom API; may require registration for detailed data) 	Annual or biennial (depending on survey cycles) 	Child mortality, vaccination, malnutrition, and related indicators. Data collected via surveys (e.g. MICS); methodology differences may exist. Collaboration with WHO/UNICEF country offices helps validate figures .

Reliable for high-level funding data, though details depend on reporting by donors.

API (HDX HAPI, free; requires one-time registration) 	Monthly (data updated regularly) 	Provides common operational datasets and indicators for Yemen (and other crises).
