This means all datasets are at population, institutional, or geographic levels (national or at most regional statistics); YETO will never ask for or show individual names, personal addresses, phone numbers, or any such private data. Even when YETO ingests documents, any that contain sensitive personal data are either excluded or redacted to remove PII. In user-facing features, the platform might allow user accounts for analysts or contributors, but those are limited to professional information and are protected by secure authentication – no public profiles revealing personal data. YETO’s privacy policy clarifies that any data contributions from partners must also be stripped of PII. For example, if an NGO partner uploads survey data, they must aggregate or anonymize responses before it enters YETO. This policy aligns with humanitarian Do No Harm principles: beyond legal compliance, YETO considers the safety implications of data. It will not publish sensitive geolocation details that could endanger individuals or communities. For instance, if a dataset on IDP camp locations is available, YETO would ensure it’s presented in a way that cannot be used to target those populations (e.g. only general area or governorate-level, not GPS coordinates of small settlements). When in doubt, YETO errs on the side of caution and either aggregates further or withholds data that, while not personal, could be indirectly identifying (like a data point that only one village would have). The Privacy Policy statement on the site clearly informs users that YETO does not handle personal data and thus falls outside personal data regulations for the most part, but it still commits to protecting any user-provided info (like login email addresses) with best practices (encryption at rest, etc.). It also mentions that any feedback or inquiries are stored without personal identifiers unless voluntarily provided. YETO’s internal logs and analytics likewise avoid any personal data tracking – e.g., no IP addresses are shown publicly, no tracking of individual user behavior beyond aggregate usage stats for improving the service.

Do‑No‑Harm Compliance: As a project focusing on a conflict-affected country, YETO follows Do No Harm guidelines closely. This is reflected by not just excluding PII, but also carefully considering the impacts of the data it publishes. The policy explicitly states that YETO will never publish information that could be used to foment violence, target vulnerable groups, or facilitate wrongdoing . For example, YETO might have data on checkpoints or conflict events, but it will not provide any tactical details that bad actors could misuse. In line with this, YETO also does not provide real-time granular tracking of things like troop movements or individual humanitarian worker locations – the focus is on economic and social indicators at an analytical level. The Privacy & Do No Harm section of documentation assures that even data like population needs or aid delivery locations are presented in aggregate (e.g. number of aid deliveries per district, rather than pinpointing a specific village repeatedly receiving aid). This prevents potential stigmatization or targeting of communities perceived as getting more aid, for instance. Additionally, any scenario in which data might inadvertently reveal someone’s identity (for example, an extremely small sample survey in a remote area) is flagged and considered for suppression or generalization. YETO’s team undergoes a review for sensitivity: data stewards are trained to spot and escalate any dataset that might pose ethical issues. The platform includes a disclaimer that while it strives to adhere to UN OCHA’s humanitarian data standards and ‘Do No Harm’, users should also exercise judgement in how they use the information. YETO disclaims any responsibility if someone were to misuse publicly available data, but by design it tries to ensure nothing on the site is truly damaging if interpreted in good faith. In summary, the privacy policy combined with do-no-harm means YETO is a safe source of information: it empowers analysis and decision-making without exposing anyone to risk.

Sanctions & Compliance Statement: YETO provides data on economic conditions which inevitably intersect with international sanctions regimes. The platform includes a section that lists sanctions relevant to Yemen (such as UN Security Council sanctions on certain individuals or entities, US OFAC sanctions, EU and UK sanctions lists). This is done purely for informational transparency – for example, in a context where one analyzes the banking sector, it’s relevant to know some banks or persons might be under sanctions. However, YETO does not provide any advice or facilitation regarding sanctions. The compliance statement on the site makes it clear: presenting sanctions information is not an endorsement or guidance on how to navigate them. YETO includes a legal notice that says, in effect, “Information on sanctioned entities is provided for public knowledge. YETO does not support or assist in any activity intended to evade or violate sanctions. Nothing on this platform constitutes legal advice.” This is important to avoid any perception that the platform could be used to circumvent restrictions. YETO explicitly refrains from analysis like “how to reroute trade to avoid sanctions” – such queries would be beyond its ethical scope (and the AI assistant is instructed per policy to refuse any user requests that look like seeking evasion tactics). The site’s Terms of Service likely also reinforce that users must comply with all applicable laws (including sanctions) and cannot rely on YETO as a compliance tool. On pages where sanctions data is displayed, there may be a small disclaimer note like “Source: UN/OFAC sanctions list – for reference only. YETO does not provide legal guidance.” Any data that overlaps with sanctions (e.g. if a certain company’s info is shown, and that company is sanctioned) will have a clear tag or warning indicating such status. Internally, YETO ensures that if any data provider has restrictions due to sanctions (for example, if an API becomes unavailable because of sanction rules), that’s handled appropriately and documented. In summary, YETO stays on the right side of the law by providing factual data about sanctions but without crossing into advice or judgement – it’s part of the platform’s neutrality and transparency stance.

Content Licensing & Attribution: YETO aggregates data and content from numerous sources, each with their own licensing terms. The platform honors all content licenses and has a built-in system to ensure attribution and usage conditions are met. In the Methodology/Policies documentation, there is a section listing how different source types are handled: For open data sources (like World Bank, IMF, UN datasets), YETO includes the required attribution on any page where their data appears. For instance, if World Bank’s GDP data is shown, somewhere on that chart or the metadata it will say “Source: World Bank – World Development Indicators (CC BY 4.0)” to comply with the Creative Commons license. If multiple sources are combined, all are credited. The platform even stores the exact attribution text in the source registry (e.g. some datasets from World Bank require “© World Bank, used under CC-BY 4.0”). For Creative Commons licensed textual content or images, YETO follows the specifics (attributing author, title, license). The manual details that these attributions should be visible but not intrusive – e.g. a small info icon or a footer note on visualization. For proprietary or restricted sources, special rules are documented: for example, ACLED data (if licensed for YETO) comes with an explicit prohibition on bulk public redistribution. The platform will therefore display ACLED-derived insights only in limited forms (such as aggregated statistics, or a map of events without offering full dataset downloads), and it will clearly mark “Data © ACLED. Redistribution not permitted.” Similarly for Janes or any subscription content, YETO will not expose the raw data or any material violating their terms. In the admin portal, each source entry has a “License” field that notes if data is public domain, CC BY/CC BY-SA, Non-commercial, or Proprietary – internal. The documentation instructs administrators to configure the front-end behavior based on this: e.g. if a dataset is proprietary, ensure any export function is disabled or limited to authorized users, and that a user viewing that data sees a notice like “Data provided under license, for YETO analysis only.” When YETO ingests documents (like research papers), it respects copyrights by not hosting full PDFs for download unless they are public domain or have permission. Instead, YETO might show summaries and then a link to the original source. The policy also outlines how YETO handles Share-Alike data: if some data were under CC BY-SA, any combined or derivative dataset YETO publishes might also need to be CC BY-SA – the governance plan would ensure compliance by offering an open download with attribution, fulfilling the share-alike requirement. The bottom line printed in the policy: each dataset page on YETO will have a citation and any license note required, fulfilling obligations to data providers and ensuring users know what they can and cannot do with the data. For example, a user seeing a chart can find text like “Data source: Central Bank of Yemen via YETO. Use of this data is for informational purposes; original data © Yemen Central Bank.” The admin guidance highlights sources like ACLED and Janes with bold tags in the registry (perhaps a red “Restricted” label) to remind that these should not be pushed to public without careful consideration. Also, if there are any embargoes or special permissions (e.g. a partner provides data to YETO that can be used in analysis but not shared raw for a certain time window), those are recorded and respected. This content licensing documentation ensures YETO remains a good citizen in the open data community and maintains trust with providers, which is especially important to keep data flowing.

2.3 Governance & Data Stewardship

Roles and Responsibilities: YETO’s operations rely on clear roles for team members and contributors, each with defined duties to maintain data quality and platform integrity:
	•	Data Stewards – These are domain experts responsible for specific thematic areas or data categories. For example, there might be a data steward for “Macroeconomy & Finance” and another for “Humanitarian & Social data.” Their job is to oversee all sources and datasets in their domain: reviewing the incoming data for accuracy, making sure updates occur as scheduled, and resolving any data quality issues. When the automated system finds a new source or a data gap, the relevant steward gets notified (e.g. the Trade data steward sees new “trade source” suggestions). They also handle manual data entries if needed (for instance, if a small local dataset must be added by hand, a steward will input and document it). Stewards are essentially the first line of quality control: they verify unusual values flagged by the system (like if inflation jumps wildly, they confirm if that’s real or an error), and they write summary notes or caveats that end users see (like “Note: Data for 2020 is an estimate due to conflict disruptions”). They also help maintain the glossary for terms in their field, and coordinate with translators to ensure specialized terms are correctly translated. Part of their responsibility is to assign or adjust reliability grades for datasets as conditions change (e.g. if a source’s quality deteriorates, they might downgrade it from A to B in the registry). Finally, stewards are content curators: for the Research library, a steward might select important new reports to feature. In sum, data stewards ensure the substance of YETO’s data is sound and well-explained.
	•	Administrators – Admins have overarching control of the YETO system. They manage user accounts/permissions (e.g. approving new partner contributor sign-ups, managing subscriptions for premium users), configure system settings, and have access to all admin portal features. Administrators also oversee the source registry management (they can add or remove sources manually, and edit source metadata like licensing or tags). They monitor the ingestion pipeline health via the admin dashboards (checking the ingestion monitor for failures , reviewing logs) and ensure backups and security measures are in place. In cases of content that might be sensitive or need executive decision (for example, whether to publish a dataset that might be politically contentious), the admin coordinates with the team and possibly with the sponsoring organization (Causeway) for guidance. They also ensure the platform’s governance policies (like those in this documentation) are followed by everyone. Administrators handle the corrections workflow: when a user submits a data correction or an issue, an admin ensures a ticket is created and assigned, and later that an official correction note is published if needed. Another key admin role is version control – if a major change to a dataset or definition is made, the admin approves the updated versioning and the publishing of a changelog entry on the Transparency page. Essentially, admins are the gatekeepers of the system’s proper functioning and compliance.
	•	QA Reviewers – These may be a subset of data stewards or a dedicated quality assurance team. Their focus is on systematically reviewing data quality outputs. They check the coverage scorecards and data quality dashboards that the system generates (for example, if a coverage scorecard shows that a particular indicator only has data up to 2018, they question why and whether a gap ticket exists; or if an outlier detection flagged 5 points in various series, the QA reviewer verifies each one whether it’s a true outlier or a data error). QA reviewers also double-check translations on a sample basis (ensuring that the bilingual content meets the quality bar). They handle user feedback that indicates possible errors: e.g., if a user reports “the number for X in 2019 seems wrong,” the QA person will investigate and coordinate with the relevant data steward to address it. In practice, data stewards and QA roles might overlap, but the key is that QA provides a second pair of eyes to catch anything the automated system or first-line steward might miss. The governance manual suggests a routine (say monthly QA audit meetings) where QA reviewers present any systematic issues (like “We’ve noticed source Y is frequently late – maybe we need a backup source”).
	•	Translators (Language and Glossary Managers) – These are bilingual experts (Arabic/English) who ensure that all content is translated accurately and consistently. They review the machine translations, update the controlled glossary as new terms arise, and approve the final bilingual content. In many cases, data stewards might draft content in one language (for example, an explanatory paragraph on methodology in English) and translators will produce the Arabic version (or vice versa) with fidelity to meaning and tone. They also help with localizing the UI text or any automated messages to make sure the Arabic phrasing is natural. Another duty is to handle any user-submitted suggestions about terminology – for instance, if users note that a certain economic term could be translated better, the translators consider that and possibly update the glossary. By documenting this role, YETO ensures that the translation process has accountability – it’s not left solely to AI or non-experts; professionals are in the loop to maintain the platform’s credibility in both languages.
	•	Developers/IT – The technical team (developers, data engineers, IT support) maintains the platform’s code, infrastructure, and technical roadmap. They are responsible for implementing connectors to new sources (when a source is approved, a developer may need to code the API integration or scraping script if it’s complex). They also handle scaling the infrastructure, performing updates, and ensuring security protocols are up to date. When data stewards or admins encounter pipeline errors or bugs (perhaps an ingestion script breaks due to a website change), the developers troubleshoot and fix it. They also implement new features or improvements requested by the team – for example, if stewards need a new type of data visualization or a change in how data is stored, the developers analyze and execute that. In governance terms, developers must adhere to the strict no-hallucination, no-fabrication rule in anything AI-related they implement , and ensure that all source code and data processes are documented (which ties back to auto-generating documentation). They often work closely with QA to write automated tests that cover the requirements (ensuring that every requirement is implemented and traceable). Though not directly interacting with content on the site, their role is crucial in enforcing policies in code – e.g. implementing the rule that no physical address appears on the site , or that if data is missing, the UI says “Not available yet” and triggers the gap logic . The methodology and governance docs mandate things like code versioning, PR reviews referencing requirement IDs, etc., which developers follow to keep the system robust and auditable.

New Source Review Workflow: YETO employs a dynamic Source Discovery Engine that continuously scouts for new potential data sources (be it a new dataset on HDX, a newly published report series, an NGO releasing stats, etc.)  . When the system or a team member identifies a new source, it doesn’t go straight into the live site. Instead, it enters a “Review Queue” in the admin portal . The Governance Manual describes this process step-by-step:
	1.	Automated Discovery Intake: On a weekly basis, the system adds entries to the review queue with basic metadata – e.g. “Source Name: XYZ, Publisher: Ministry of X, Type: PDF report, Initial credibility score: B, summary: contains quarterly economic stats for region Y.” These suggestions can also come from users or team members (there might be a form where someone suggests a new source, which then appears in the queue).
	2.	Data Steward Evaluation: A data steward (or a designated “Source Manager”) inspects each queue entry. They look at key criteria: Is the source credible? (Does the publisher have authority or a good track record? The system might even provide a suggested credibility rating based on known info .) Is it relevant to YETO’s scope? (e.g. A dataset must cover Yemen or a comparable country for benchmarking, and be on an economic or humanitarian topic that fits one of the thematic portals.) What are the coverage and frequency? (If it’s a one-off report versus a regularly updated feed, the approach differs.) The steward also checks licensing notes provided (the engine might parse if the source site says “data is © 2025 XYZ Ministry”). If the source requires some special handling – e.g. it’s an API that needs an API key or a login, or it’s a dataset that’s not open access – the steward notes that.
	3.	Decision – Approve or Reject: After evaluation, the steward chooses to approve the source, reject it, or mark it “needs info/decision”. Approval means they deem it credible, useful, and feasible to integrate. They will then fill in the details in the Source Registry entry: categorize it under one or multiple thematic portals, set the update frequency (cadence), assign a reliability grade (initially), and note any specific instructions (like “requires manual cleanup of PDF”). If an API key or partner agreement is needed, they mark the source as “Needs Key/Agreement” in the registry. YETO’s governance specifies that in such cases, an automated notification or task is generated for the Partnership Unit (or whoever handles external partnerships) to acquire the necessary permission or key. For example, if the source is ACLED and requires a subscription license, the system would flag “License needed” and the partnership focal point is alerted to handle the contract or API key retrieval. The source can remain in a “pending” state until the key is secured; only then do developers activate the connector. If the steward rejects the source, they provide a reason (maybe “Source was a blog with unverified data” or “Not relevant to current scope”). The system will record this, so the same source isn’t auto-suggested repeatedly. (Rejected sources could be reviewed periodically in case circumstances change, but they won’t clutter the queue.)
	4.	Integration of Approved Source: Once approved (and keys/credentials in place if needed), the source is officially added to the Master Source Registry. The admin interface allows the steward or admin to hit “Activate,” which triggers the setup of an ingestion pipeline. At this point, a developer might be brought in if a new connector script is required (the manual instructs: if the source is one of known standard formats like CKAN API or RSS feed, perhaps no coding is needed beyond configuration; if it’s completely new, devs will implement accordingly). The source is tagged with relevant topics so that it automatically populates the right pages (for instance, a source providing “Fuel import volumes” might be tagged for Trade & Commerce and Energy & Fuel pages). The scheduling is also set up (e.g. fetch this source monthly on the 5th day).
	5.	Monitoring and Re-evaluation: After initial integration, stewards keep an eye on the first few runs – if the data fetched seems off or incomplete, they might tweak parameters. The governance policy says that every source entry should have a review date – e.g. once a year, confirm that source is still active and valuable. Also, if an approved source starts failing (say an API goes down or data quality drops), a steward can decide to disable or put it back into review (and possibly seek an alternative source).

This workflow ensures no source is blindly ingested without human vetting, which protects against incorrect or malicious data entering the platform. It also keeps the source catalog expanding in a controlled, auditable way (each addition is logged, with who approved it and when). The documentation highlights that the Source Discovery Engine is quite broad – scanning academic databases, humanitarian data portals, etc.  – so stewards might get a lot of suggestions, but they are expected to prioritize those filling obvious gaps first (the system might aid this by highlighting gap areas). The review process is a cornerstone of YETO’s governance model, preventing both omissions (by finding new sources) and commissions (by filtering out bad data sources).

Data Gap Ticket System: YETO’s Data Gap Ticket system is a key governance tool to maintain up-to-date coverage. The documentation outlines how it works and how stewards handle these tickets:
	•	Auto-Generation of Tickets: The platform automatically creates a Gap Ticket in two main scenarios: (1) A scheduled update did not occur on time. For instance, a dataset that should be monthly hasn’t received new data for 3 months past its last expected date – the system flags this, e.g. “Gap: No new data for Consumer Price Index since July 2025, expected monthly.” (2) A user or team member manually flags an issue via the interface (“Report an issue” button). For example, a user might report “The value for unemployment in 2023 is missing or seems incorrect.” This feedback creates a ticket attached to that dataset/indicator. All gap tickets have a unique ID, timestamp, and relevant metadata (which dataset/indicator, timeframe of missing data, etc.).
	•	Ticket Details and Priority: Each ticket includes the context the system can provide: the missing item and its importance . It might auto-suggest possible reasons or sources (based on the configuration info – e.g. if the source is an API that is currently returning an error code, that error is logged; or if the source has known delays). The ticket is categorized by priority levels: “Critical” for high-profile indicators (like exchange rate or conflict event updates) and lower priority for less critical or infrequent data. The governance guidelines set some default priorities; for example, failure to update the Dashboard KPI indicators would be high priority, whereas a missing minor sub-indicator might be medium.
	•	Notification & Assignment: When a ticket is generated, the relevant data steward and administrators get a notification (likely via email or the admin dashboard highlighting open tickets). If the gap pertains to a specific source, it’s assigned to the steward responsible for that source’s category. For instance, a gap in a health survey would go to the “Humanitarian/Social Data Steward.” The Partnership Unit might also be notified if the gap is due to an external partner not delivering data as expected (as the user specifically requested: a unique notification to partnership for data agreement issues). For example, if a partner NGO was supposed to upload a dataset by a certain date and it didn’t happen, the system would notify the partnership coordinator to follow up with that NGO.
	•	Triaging and Investigation: The data steward reviews the ticket and begins investigating. The manual provides a flowchart or list: first, check if the source itself has published new data on their end. It could be that the source changed their format or endpoint – in that case, involve a developer to adjust the connector (this would be logged as the resolution: “Fixed API URL, re-run ingestion, data obtained”). Or the source simply hasn’t released the data – perhaps a delay due to crisis – then the steward notes that. They might temporarily mark the dataset on the front-end with a note “Update delayed, expected by X if possible.” The steward also evaluates if an alternate source can fill the gap. YETO’s design encourages multiple sources, so maybe another dataset could serve as a proxy. For instance, if the official CPI is not updated, maybe WFP’s food price index is used as a stopgap (with appropriate grade adjustment from A to C). The ticket would document such decisions, and possibly spawn a task: “consider integrating source Z as interim data – see Source Review Queue.”
	•	Resolution and Documentation: Once the cause is found and addressed, the steward resolves the ticket. Resolutions could be: “Data updated successfully on [date] from source (the pipeline resumed)” or “Source discontinued – marked dataset as discontinued and communicated this on platform” or “Switched to alternative source Y for this indicator.” If the resolution involved any manual data entry or correction, that is noted and a public correction note may be generated if appropriate (for transparency, if an error was fixed, YETO might publish a small note like “Corrected historical value for X on [date] due to source revision”). The governance policy says that significant corrections should generate public notes , which are visible perhaps in a changelog or on the affected data’s page. Minor gaps that are just delays might not need a public explanation beyond the existing “not available” note, but the internal log still records it.
	•	Ongoing Management: The Data Gap Ticket system is integrated with the platform’s coverage scorecard – essentially, an overview that shows what percentage of key indicators are up to date. Admins and stewards periodically review open tickets. If a ticket remains open too long, it might be escalated: e.g. if a crucial indicator has been missing for six months, maybe the team decides to escalate to management or re-evaluate the approach (are we expecting data that won’t come? do we remove the indicator? etc.). The manual defines SLA (service-level agreements) for ticket resolution based on priority: for instance, critical items should be addressed within a week if possible, medium maybe within a month, etc., acknowledging that sometimes the resolution is waiting on an external entity.

This ticket system, documented for the team, ensures that gaps don’t fall through cracks – they are systematically tracked, and users are kept aware that YETO knows about the gap and is working on it. It’s a proactive approach to maintain the platform’s integrity and completeness.

2.4 Site Map & Content Coverage

YETO’s site map is structured to cover a wide range of thematic areas and user needs, ensuring that all content is organized into intuitive sections. Below is an outline of the site’s main sections (as listed on the landing page and navigation) and a description of content coverage and data sources for each:
	•	Dashboard: The Dashboard is the entry point providing a snapshot of Yemen’s current economic situation. It features a handful of top-line KPI indicators (such as GDP growth rate, inflation rate, foreign exchange rate, foreign reserves level, and labor force participation or unemployment rate). These indicators are pulled from the most reliable sources: for example, GDP growth might come from the World Bank or IMF (or YETO’s own estimate if recent official data are absent), inflation from either official CPI (if available) or YETO’s price proxy, exchange rates from the Central Bank and market average, etc. Each KPI card on the dashboard displays the latest value, a sparkline of recent trend, and a confidence grade. The Dashboard aggregates data across sectors – it’s essentially a “greatest hits” of key stats. Data on the Dashboard is updated automatically as new source data comes in (e.g., if the monthly price index updates, the inflation KPI will refresh). The sources behind each KPI are indicated via tooltips or small text (like “Source: World Bank, last updated 2024” for GDP). This page gives policymakers and the public a quick health check of the economy in one place.
	•	Data Repository: This is the comprehensive data catalog of YETO, where users can search, filter, and download datasets. The Data Repository includes all quantitative datasets stored in YETO’s database. Users can browse by category (which correspond to the sectors and themes) or use a search bar to find a specific indicator. For example, one could filter by “Prices & Cost of Living” to see all related datasets (fuel prices, food basket costs, CPI, etc.), or search “literacy rate” or “GDP per capita.” Each dataset in the repository has a summary page with meta-information: definition, source(s), frequency, coverage period, reliability grade, and license. From that page, users can visualize the data or download it (in CSV or via an API call). Key sources that feed the repository include the World Bank and IMF data (for macro indicators and development indicators), UN agencies (for social stats), national sources (for whatever official numbers are available), as well as YETO’s own derived series. Essentially, if it’s a number or time-series used anywhere on YETO, it will be findable in the Data Repository. The repository content is kept in sync with the Master Source Registry – whenever a new series is ingested, it’s categorized and appears here. This section ensures transparency by allowing users to get the raw data behind any chart. For example, if the Banking sector page shows a chart of money supply, the repository will have the “Money Supply (M2) – Yemen – Monthly 2010-2025” dataset available, sourced perhaps from IMF’s IFS.
	•	Research & Reports: This section serves as a library of analytical reports, assessments, and papers related to Yemen’s economy and humanitarian situation. It collates both YETO’s own analyses (if any) and external publications. Sources feeding this section include the ReliefWeb API (to automatically ingest new reports from various organizations like UN, NGOs, World Bank) and possibly direct uploads by admins of important documents not on ReliefWeb (e.g. a PDF of Yemen’s national development plan if it’s public). The Research library is organized by date and topic tags. Users can read summaries of each report and then view details. Each report entry includes the title (in both English and Arabic if available), the source/author (e.g. “World Bank Yemen Economic Monitor, Fall 2025”), a short abstract or key findings, and a link to download the full report (or view it if YETO stores text). YETO also cross-references datasets and reports: for instance, a World Bank report might be linked to the data series it cites (and those series are in the Data Repository). On a report’s page, users might see an “Extract” or highlights with in-line citations linking back to data. Key types of content here: economic outlook reports, needs assessments, policy briefs, academic papers on Yemen. The platform likely also produces some summary charts for reports if applicable (like if a report has a graph, YETO might recreate it interactively using the data). This section ensures that qualitative insights and context accompany the raw data on the site. The Latest Updates feed is also part of this section (as mentioned in nav: e.g., a blog-style feed of short updates or news). That feed might be auto-generated by combining new data releases and snippets from reports. For example, if Central Bank announces a policy change, or WFP releases a new market alert, an update card is created in the feed, linking to sources and data. Sources for that could be an RSS feed or manual entry by stewards, but all with citations. In summary, Research & Reports is the narrative complement to the numbers – providing depth and explanation.
	•	Economic Sector Pages: Under the “Sectors” section, YETO has dedicated pages for various thematic areas of the economy, each functioning like a mini-dashboard/library focused on that theme. These pages synthesize data from multiple sources specific to the topic, and present key indicators, trends, and relevant documents. The expanded registry ensures that each of these pages is populated with data relevant to the theme. Below are the sector pages (as planned) and their coverage:
	•	Trade & Commerce: This page covers Yemen’s trade flows and commercial activity. Key indicators likely include total exports and imports (sourced from UN Comtrade or national customs if available), trade balance, and composition of trade (maybe top 5 commodities exported/imported). It might also show port statistics (like port throughput if data from port authorities or UNVIM (verification mechanism) is available for shipment volumes). Sources: UN Comtrade for historical trade data, possibly UNCTADstat or IMF DOTS for any recent trade estimates, and any national reports on commerce (like chamber of commerce bulletins). If the war disrupted official trade data, YETO might use mirror data (what trading partners report). The page could have a chart of trade volume over time, a breakdown pie chart of exports by category (oil vs non-oil, for example), and highlight any trade-related events (e.g. blockade periods or policy changes). It will also link to relevant research like World Bank or WTO analyses on Yemen trade. In summary, the Trade & Commerce page consolidates all trade indicators, from container traffic to export earnings, giving a picture of how open or isolated the economy is.
	•	Local Economy: The “Local Economy” page focuses on internal market activity and the everyday economic conditions in Yemen’s towns and cities. This could include market prices of essential goods (staple food prices, perhaps visualized via maps or tables for different governorates – data from WFP’s market monitoring is key here), business activity indicators (if any surveys of local business confidence or activity exist), and possibly exchange rates in local markets. Since “Local Economy” was hinted as focusing on markets, the WFP Market Monitor data is central: showing trends in the price of wheat, flour, fuel, etc., across various cities. It may also display an index of purchasing power, like Terms of Trade between labor wages and food prices (WFP often calculates how many kilograms of wheat flour can be bought with a day’s wage – a measure of household purchasing power). Also included might be any available data on local market functionality (e.g. number of marketplaces open, from FEWSNET or others). News or anecdotal reports about local economic conditions can be featured – for example, a blurb from a Sanaa Center Economic Bulletin about market shortages can be shown with a link. Sources beyond WFP could include FEWS NET reports or ILO if any labor wage data, or Yemen Chamber of Commerce surveys if such exist. The Local Economy page essentially grounds the macro data to the street level: how are prices and livelihoods faring in marketplaces from Sana’a to Aden to Mukalla.
	•	Rural Development: This page highlights agriculture and rural livelihoods. Yemen’s rural economy (farming, livestock, fishing, rural infrastructure) would be covered here. Key indicators: agricultural production (e.g. volume of sorghum, coffee, qat – data might come from FAOSTAT or Ministry of Agriculture reports pre-war, and FAO estimates during the war), rainfall or crop yields if available (maybe from FEWSNET or remote sensing proxies), rural population livelihood indicators (like % of population in rural areas with access to services, or number of rural employment programs via Social Fund for Development). Also, development projects in rural areas can be covered: data from IATI on projects tagged as rural or from the Social Fund for Development (SFD) (the SFD might have data on number of projects, beneficiaries in rural communities). The page might show, for example, a chart of agricultural GDP over time (if we have it from World Bank data), or livestock population trends (perhaps FAO data), and include maps of things like vegetation index (NDVI) or an infographic from FAO. Reports from FAO, WFP, or Ministry of Agriculture would be linked. This page ensures that the often-neglected rural economy is separately visible, considering Yemen’s large rural population and issues like irrigation, land, etc.
	•	Banking & Finance: This page focuses on the financial sector. It compiles data on currency, banking system health, and financial proxies. Indicators and content include: Exchange rates (official vs parallel – possibly shown as a dual chart or a widening gap over time), monetary indicators like money supply (M1/M2 from IMF IFS if available), inflation or price of money (if any interest rate or informal inflation proxy), banking sector data such as number of functioning banks, credit to private sector (World Bank or central bank data if any), microfinance outreach (from Yemen Microfinance Network as we have in sources, showing e.g. number of microfinance borrowers by year), and any financial inclusion data (like percentage of adults with bank accounts, perhaps from Global Findex or anecdotal). It may also include sanctions info relevant to banking: e.g. listing banks under sanctions or restrictions on Yemeni financial institutions (this is why sources include sanctions lists – those could be highlighted here, like a sidebar “Sanctioned Banks/Individuals”). News bulletins relevant to banking are included – e.g. Central Bank policy changes (interest rate, new currency printed, etc.) or events like moving the Central Bank headquarters. If available, data on remittances (an important financial inflow for Yemen) would be here too – maybe from World Bank migration and remittances data, or proxy via balance of payments from IMF. In short, this page draws from Central Bank reports, IMF data, microfinance reports, and market data to provide a picture of the monetary and banking system. For instance, a graph might show the divergence of official vs market exchange rate over time with annotations for major events (like injection of new banknotes). The Banking & Finance page helps users monitor currency stability, liquidity, and the impact of conflict on financial institutions.
	•	Currency & Exchange: While related to banking, this page zeroes in specifically on currency metrics. Given Yemen’s context, it might emphasize daily exchange rate trackers in various regions (the platform could show interactive charts for, say, Sana’a vs Aden rates in YER/USD, including the introduction of new banknotes etc.). It also can show currency in circulation (if known), and inflation rate specifically if that’s more about currency value (though inflation might also be under Prices & Cost of Living). This page might also incorporate money transfer rates (the mention of “FX: official + parallel + transfer; north vs south” in requirements  suggests tracking the black market rate and perhaps the money transfer (hawala) rate which often lies in between official and cash market rates). Sources: Central Bank for official, market surveys for parallel, possibly money exchange feeds (some projects collect daily exchange rates from exchange offices). Also, any currency policy announcements (like introducing a new denomination, or a devaluation decision) would be noted here or on the timeline. Essentially, this is a dedicated hub for anyone particularly interested in Yemeni Rial’s stability and value. It likely overlaps with Banking & Finance content, but is separated to highlight the currency issue (which is a headline issue in Yemen’s economy).
	•	Food Security: This page compiles indicators on availability, access, and utilization of food in Yemen – a critical humanitarian metric. Key content: Food price indices (like the cost of minimum food basket, trend over time, from WFP), household food security surveys (e.g. percentage of households with poor food consumption, from WFP or FSAC cluster reports), Malnutrition rates (from UNICEF/WHO – e.g. GAM rates among children, if available in surveys), Integrated Phase Classification (IPC) results if they are published (these classify severity of food insecurity across regions – often maps or pie charts of population in IPC Phase 3/4/5). Data sources include WFP (for market prices and perhaps Food Security Outcome Monitoring), FAO (for any crop production or food import dependency stats), UNICEF (for nutrition), and FSAC Cluster or IPC reports (for composite measures). The page could show a map with latest IPC classification by area, a chart of how many people are in need over time (often OCHA or IPC reports give “people in acute food insecurity” numbers year by year). It also might integrate food aid delivery data – e.g. amount of food distributed by WFP monthly (if WFP publishes that). The Food Security page contextualizes economic data in terms of human impact: even if GDP or market prices are one thing, how many people are going hungry is captured here. It will feature links to major reports like FAO’s Food Security updates, WFP bulletins, etc., and data visualizations like a timeline of food price spikes corresponding to conflict events (for instance, showing how wheat flour price jumped during a port blockade). This page draws heavily from WFP’s vulnerability analysis data and UNOCHA needs assessments.
	•	Energy & Fuel: This sector page deals with fuel, energy supply, and infrastructure related to energy. Key indicators: Fuel prices (gasoline, diesel – likely from Fuel Price Bulletins or WFP data as we have), fuel import volumes (maybe from official sources or UNVIM which tracks ships carrying fuel into Yemen), electricity access (perhaps % of population with electricity access, from WB or WHO surveys), power generation output (if data from electricity corporations exist, likely not regularly, but possibly occasional stats like “available power MW”). It might also cover cooking gas availability and price. Sources: Yemen Petroleum Company (for official price set in the north, and the Aden government for south – we have those bulletins as sources), UNOCHA/UNVIM (monthly reports on fuel imports into Hudaydah etc.), World Bank or UNDP for any broader energy stats (e.g. installed capacity). Additionally, news: e.g. reports of fuel shortages, attacks on energy infrastructure (which could also appear on timeline). Visualizations might include fuel price trend lines (north vs south), bar charts of fuel import quantities over months, and maybe a pie of electricity source (but Yemen grid is minimal, so likely focusing on fuel for generators). The Energy & Fuel page thus centralizes how the fuel supply (vital for transportation and generators) is affecting the economy – linking with other sectors like showing how fuel price hikes feed into cost of living.
	•	Aid Flows: This page tracks international aid into Yemen, providing a financial perspective on humanitarian and development assistance. It aggregates data from OCHA FTS (for humanitarian aid funding) and IATI (for broader development projects and aid activities). Key indicators: Total humanitarian funding per year (with a gauge of % of appeals met), top donors and top recipient organizations, aid by sector (how much goes to food, health, etc.), possibly development vs humanitarian split. Visual elements could include a running total for the current year’s funding (e.g. “2026 Humanitarian Response Plan: 70% funded, $1.4bn of $2bn appealed”), a bar chart comparing funding across years 2010–present (showing spikes during conflict years), and maps of aid projects (if granular data from IATI like project locations). YETO might create donor profile pages and implementer pages  – so the Aid Flows page might link to those (for example, clicking on “USA” donor shows a profile with how much USA gave each year, and maybe related news). The sources are primarily FTS API (for humanitarian $) and IATI data (for development aid, project counts, etc.). There might also be some content from World Bank’s Yemen portfolio (like number of active WB projects and disbursements). The page likely also surfaces relevant analysis: e.g. a chart or note on “aid per capita” or “aid as % of GDP”. It provides accountability by listing content licensing for data (the policy says content license obligations for sources like FTS which is open data anyway). The Aid Flows page answers questions like: Who is funding Yemen’s relief and how is it trending? Which sectors are underfunded? and links to reports such as OCHA humanitarian updates or donor reports.
	•	Poverty & Development: This page focuses on human development indicators and poverty metrics. It likely shows poverty headcount ratio (the percentage of population below the poverty line – historically ~48% in 2014; for later years it might rely on estimates by UNDP or WB modeling due to lack of surveys). It also includes Human Development Index (HDI) components for Yemen (life expectancy, education, GNI per capita – from UNDP), literacy rates, school enrollment, health indicators like child mortality or access to clean water (World Bank or UNICEF sources). Essentially, broader socio-economic indicators beyond just income. The page might chart any data available on poverty over time or comparisons across regions if there was any pre-war data by governorate. It will note data gaps (e.g. “No recent national poverty survey; numbers are estimates”). World Bank and UN agencies (UNDP, UNICEF, WHO etc.) are the main sources here. Another angle is development progress: e.g. number of people with access to electricity, or the unemployment rate (perhaps labor market overlaps but unemployment is also a dev indicator – ILOSTAT could provide a pre-war figure and then probably nothing recent). This page also might highlight impacts of the conflict on development – for example using night-light data or education enrollment drops. All related content (like UNDP’s Human Development Reports, any poverty assessments) are linked. By having this page, YETO ensures that not only emergency indicators are shown but also longer-term development metrics and how far behind they might be.
	•	Labour Market: The Labour Market page covers employment, jobs, and wages. It would present any available stats on unemployment rate (likely only pre-war official or modeled by ILO/WB), labor force participation (again from ILO or WB, possibly outdated), and wage rates. Given real data paucity, YETO might rely on proxies: for instance, wage rates for unskilled labor that WFP collects as part of its market monitoring (they often track the daily wage for casual labor and how it changes). If WFP’s data is there, the page can chart the daily wage trend in YER and how inflation has affected its real value. It can also show gender-disaggregated data if any (like female labor participation was low historically – if any new info is there, likely not, but at least note old stats). Additionally, if there are public sector salary updates (the Yemeni government and de facto authorities paying or not paying salaries is a huge issue), the page could note whether civil servants are being paid and any data on that (e.g. “% of civil servants receiving regular salary: X%”, from news or UN reports). Data sources: ILO/ILOSTAT for historical labor indicators, WFP for wage data, possibly Ministry of Civil Service announcements or Media for public salary news. Also, any micro-data like from a recent survey by e.g. UNDP on livelihoods could be used. The page would also link to reports on employment programs, e.g. “Cash-for-Work programs by SFD employed 50k people” – giving context how aid tries to support the labor market. Overall, this page acknowledges that employment info is limited but whatever is known is summarized, providing at least an indication of the labor crisis (like massive unemployment due to conflict, even if not precisely measured).
	•	Infrastructure: The Infrastructure & Services page collates data on physical infrastructure and basic services in Yemen. This could include statistics on access to services: e.g. % of population with access to clean water, sanitation, electricity (from WHO/UNICEF JMP for WASH maybe, or WB data, albeit outdated), road network status (km of roads open vs damaged, if any data from Ministry of Public Works or UNOPS projects), telecommunications (number of mobile subscribers from ITU maybe), and infrastructure damage assessments (World Bank or UNDP have conducted damage assessments in Yemen’s cities – those could be summarized e.g. “X hospitals damaged, Y schools damaged in conflict” from reports). It might also show service functionality metrics: e.g. how many health facilities operational (WHO used to track health facility functionality). Data sources are varied: UN OCHA and cluster reports for things like health facilities, World Bank for infrastructure indices, UN agencies for sectoral stats, Yemeni ministries if any publish reconstruction updates. Additionally, project data from IATI or WB could highlight how many infrastructure projects are ongoing (like number of schools built by UNICEF, etc.). A visual might be a bar chart of access rates (water, electricity) comparing pre-war and current estimates, or maps of infrastructure status if available regionally. This page likely has strong overlaps with humanitarian info, since infrastructure destruction is a big part of crisis impact. Including it ensures YETO doesn’t just have pure economic numbers but also the state of foundational systems that underpin the economy.
	•	Conflict Economy: The Conflict Economy & Political Economy page explores how the war and fragmentation have impacted economic conditions. It’s a bit more qualitative but can include data-driven insights such as conflict event counts affecting economic assets (e.g. number of attacks on oil facilities, or number of times ports were closed – from ACLED events tagged by type or specific data like UNDSS or insurance reports if any). It also can present black market indicators like fuel black market prices vs official (some data might be gleaned from reports), smuggling or illicit trade estimates (for instance, volume of fuel smuggled, arms trade value – likely from think tank reports or UN Panel of Experts). YETO might have to rely on narrative and proxies here: for example, an index of “economic activity in conflict zones” which could use nighttime lights in areas of active conflict to show how war darkens the economy. There might also be a fragmentation of institutions table: e.g. two central banks, two telecom authorities, etc., to illustrate the parallel economies emerging – such info would come from political economy analysis reports (Sana’a Center, etc.). This page will also show sanctions impact – perhaps summarizing how many Yemeni entities are sanctioned (from the sanctions data) and any effects (like humanitarian exemptions etc.). It likely integrates with the timeline to highlight major conflict-related economic events: e.g. the Central Bank split in 2016, the fuel subsidy cuts, the creation of a new currency in Aden, etc. Sources feeding this: ACLED for conflict events, UN Panel of Experts reports (for detailed war economy stuff, albeit narrative), Sana’a Center Economic Bulletins or Chatham House reports for analyses. Because much of conflict economy data is qualitative or estimated, YETO presents what it can as data (like event counts, sanctions counts) and links out to deeper analysis. The page emphasizes that the war has shaped all other sectors – tying together threads from other pages but focusing on war-specific phenomena (like diversion of public funds, emergence of new trading networks). Warnings will be included (per the do-no-harm rule) – e.g. certain data on conflict economy might be redacted or generalized to avoid sensitivity (the governance note “no enabling harm” is relevant here  ).
	•	Public Finance: The Public Finance & Governance page presents data on government revenues, expenditures, and fiscal policy. Even though Yemen’s official budgets are disrupted, any info on public finances is crucial. Indicators may include government revenue (perhaps customs revenue, oil export revenue, tax collection – fragmented between north and south; sources could be IMF reports or Ministry of Finance releases if any, or Yemen Economic Bulletin estimates), government expenditures (e.g. salary payments as fraction of budget, spending on services, again from reports), fiscal deficit or gap, and public debt (some figures from WB/IMF about debt stock or arrears). If available, data on execution of budgets in both north and south could be shown (maybe as two columns for comparison if data exists from official statements). This page also notes public finance governance: e.g. whether audits are happening, or how transparent budgets are (possibly using indices like PEFA if Yemen had one, or open budget index pre-war). Additionally, any policy announcements like new taxes, tariff changes, or public wage increases would be listed. Data sources: IMF Article IV reports (they often have a table of fiscal operations with estimates for recent years), World Bank for public expenditure review data if any, official budgets (if the recognized government publishes an annual budget PDF, YETO can extract what’s planned vs actual). The timeline integration is key: policy events like the announcement of currency devaluation, introduction of customs in both north and south, etc., are highlighted. This page gives users insight into how the government(s) are financed (or struggling to finance), and the economic governance aspects. It likely includes narrative explanation because raw data might be sparse. The data registry ensures that any figure shown (like “Oil revenue in 2021: X million USD”) has a citation, e.g. “Source: Yemen Ministry of Finance via IMF”. If certain data is not officially available, YETO might cite secondary sources (like an ACAPS report saying “only 30% of civil servants are paid”). All such content will be clearly sourced given the sensitivity.
	•	Investment & Private Sector: This page is about business and investment climate, including both domestic private sector activity and foreign direct investment (FDI). It could show FDI inflows (if any, from UNCTAD or WB), number of new businesses registered per year (maybe from Ministry of Trade or WB’s Doing Business data up to 2015), business sentiment indices if any (e.g. a survey by a chamber of commerce). It may also highlight key industries (like oil production data – though that could be here or in Trade or Public Finance, but as investment, maybe note oil sector investment or output), and investment projects (for example, tracking major projects financed by Gulf countries or World Bank in infrastructure – somewhat overlapping with Aid Flows and Infrastructure). Another interesting dataset: remittances from abroad – Yemen’s economy heavily relies on expat remittances. World Bank has modelled data for remittances; if available, that would be shown as part of private financial inflows. Sources: UNCTAD World Investment Report for FDI (pre-war Yemen had small FDI, war likely near zero aside maybe telecoms), Central Bank reports for any mention of remittance figures, World Bank for private sector surveys or enterprise statistics (they did enterprise surveys pre-war). The page might also use nightlights or other proxies to indicate where private economic activity is happening (e.g. an interactive map of night-time light intensity in industrial zones). Reports from organizations like the Yemen Chambers of Commerce or international economic think tanks on Yemen’s private sector would be referenced. This page essentially tries to answer: Is anyone investing in Yemen? How are local businesses doing? For instance, it might mention the state of the banking sector lending to private business (which is near halt) or how many factories are operational (if an assessment from UNIDO or others exists). All in all, it’s a cross-cutting page that complements Trade and Finance by focusing specifically on the private sector lens.
	•	Timeline: The Timeline is an interactive chronological list of significant events from 2010 to present that have economic relevance. It merges events from multiple sources into one sequence. Users can filter events by categories such as Conflict, Policy, Aid, Sanctions, Natural Disaster, etc. Key sources for events include ACLED (for conflict events: battles, explosions, etc., which YETO might filter to show only those with economic impact like attacks on oil pipelines or major shifts in control of economic hubs), UCDP (for overarching conflict milestones by year), UN Sanctions updates (each time a person or entity is sanctioned, and major UNSC resolutions), policy announcements (this may come from scraping news or official statements – e.g. Central Bank decisions, government economic reforms), and economic shocks like currency devaluations or fuel price hikes (the system might treat a sudden 30% drop in currency as an “event” and mark it on timeline). The timeline likely also includes humanitarian events like “Yemen HRP launched, asking $X billion” or “Donor conference pledged $Y billion” because those affect the economy. Each event entry on the timeline has a title, date, and brief description, often with a citation. For example: “Aug 2016: Central Bank of Yemen headquarters relocated from Sana’a to Aden【source】 – this led to a split in monetary authority.” Or “June 2018: Hodeidah Port offensive begins【source】 – causing a temporary closure of Yemen’s largest port (70% of imports).” The timeline events are linked to data: users can click an event and see related indicators (the UI might highlight that after the Aug 2016 CBY move, the exchange rate divergence accelerated – and provide a quick link to the currency chart). Conversely, on a data chart, certain events might be annotated (e.g. a note on the exchange rate chart marking “Aug 2016: CBY relocation”). Sources for the timeline besides ACLED and sanction lists include ReliefWeb updates (for major humanitarian announcements), local news (if systematically ingested or curated), and UN reports (e.g. “Stockholm Agreement signed – includes paying civil servant salaries” in Dec 2018). YETO’s data stewards and the AI assistant can also inject events when needed – if an unusual data outlier is detected with no known event, they investigate and possibly add “discovered event: e.g. flood in Hadramout destroyed crops in spring 2021”. The timeline is presented in both English and Arabic, with an interface to scroll or jump by year. This feature allows users to contextualize data within the narrative of Yemen’s recent history, embodying YETO’s integrated approach (data + narrative). Because of the variety of sources, each event entry shows its reference (ACLED for conflict events, UN press release for policy events, etc.), maintaining credibility.
	•	Glossary & Methodology: This section (or page) provides users with the definitions of terms, acronyms, and indicators used on the platform, as well as an explanation of YETO’s methodology (essentially a user-friendly summary drawn from the Methodology Manual). In practice, it may be split into two tabs: Glossary (an alphabetical list of key terms in both languages) and Methodology (text and diagrams explaining how YETO works). The Glossary part contains entries like “GDP (Gross Domestic Product) – The total value of goods and services produced… (with Arabic equivalent definition), Source: CSO or WB, note on base year etc.” or “Food Insecurity Phase 4 – Definition: Emergency level of food insecurity as per IPC classification.” It’s a controlled vocabulary so that users and even the translation engine use consistent language. The Methodology part likely highlights the points from 2.1 in a more concise way for the public: describing how data is collected (mentioning automated pipelines, source vetting, provenance tracking, and the confidence grades A–D with a simple legend). It will also reassure how contradictions are handled openly, and how often data is updated. Essentially, it’s a transparency page to build user trust: explaining that “Every number here has a source; if data is missing, we say so and we seek it.” There might be a section about “How to interpret YETO data” which includes understanding the reliability grades (A–D) and what a user should do if they spot an issue (likely encouraging them to report it, which ties into the corrections policy). Because it’s bilingual, this page will also note the translation approach: e.g. “All content is available in Arabic and English. We use advanced machine translation reviewed by humans to ensure accuracy.” This gives users confidence that the Arabic content is as good as the English. In summary, Glossary & Methodology is both a reference (for terms) and a guide (for how YETO maintains data integrity), supporting advanced users in interpreting the platform’s information correctly.
	•	Data Quality & Transparency: This page is dedicated to YETO’s commitment to data quality, acting as a public accountability dashboard. It likely shares some overlap with the methodology content but focuses on current status. For example, it might display the coverage scorecard – a summary of how much of the indicator set is up-to-date vs. gaps. It could include statistics like “Data coverage: 85% of core indicators updated within last 12 months; 10 known data gaps pending.” It may also list recent corrections or revisions: e.g. “(Date) – Corrected exchange rate data for Jan 2024 due to source revision.” Additionally, the page can provide transparency on sources and licensing: possibly listing all data sources with their license and a link (almost an online version of the Master Source Registry for users to see). Another section could be “Confidence and Bias”: explaining the confidence grades and listing how many series are A, B, C, D. If any data is categorized as D (disputed), the Transparency page will mention what those are and why (for instance, “GDP for 2020 has conflicting estimates – we show both, see Economy page.”). The page might also outline YETO’s corrections policy and allow users to see how to report issues. In effect, this acts as a “trust center” for the platform. Sources of info here are internal (the provenance database, the gap ticket log, etc.). YETO might automatically update parts of this page – e.g., whenever a data gap ticket is open, the number on this page updates. Ensuring this page is populated is a deliverable (it demonstrates YETO’s ethos: nothing to hide, continuous improvement). It likely links to the full Methodology and to the Legal/Policy pages for more details, but remains user-friendly (possibly FAQ style entries about data updates, source vetting etc.). By having a dedicated page, YETO meets the promise that transparency is not just in data points but in the process itself.
	•	Legal & Policies: The Legal section contains the formal documents: Privacy Policy, Terms of Service, content licensing information, and any disclaimers (like the sanctions disclaimer and do-no-harm statement). It’s the fine print that users or partners can refer to in order to understand their rights and responsibilities. The Privacy Policy subsection reiterates that no personal data is collected and describes how user data (accounts, if any) are handled. The Terms of Service (ToS) covers permissible use of the site – e.g. users should not attempt to abuse the site, the data is provided “as is” without warranty, etc., and notes jurisdiction (likely governed by international law or the operator’s country). It also might include a clause that heavy use of the API should respect rate limits and attribution. The Data License & Reuse part explicitly tells users what they can do with YETO content: since much data is from open sources, YETO would encourage use with attribution, but for combined or unique content it might specify a license (the Causeway team might license YETO’s original content under CC BY-NC or something – that detail would be in this section). The sanctions and compliance statements appear here formally: e.g. “Compliance with Laws: Users agree not to use YETO for any unlawful purposes, including sanctions evasion or activities contravening export controls.” There will also be an Accessibility note (as commonly included in legal footer) stating the site’s commitment to WCAG standards and who to contact if content is not accessible. And importantly, a Do No Harm disclaimer might be in ToS: “While YETO provides data to inform humanitarian response and research, we carefully vet content to avoid harm. Users should exercise responsibility in interpreting data in context.” The admin portal ensures compliance by highlighting licensing obligations for each source – that internal note corresponds to this public page which might have an “Attributions” list for various datasets. Essentially, the Legal & Policies page is a static but important reference for anyone concerned about how data can be used and what YETO’s responsibilities are. It likely does not have dynamic data (aside from possibly listing licenses), but it’s fully written out per requirements and kept updated if any policy changes (with version date).
	•	Admin / Contributor Portals: These aren’t public pages in the main navigation, but they are parts of the site architecture accessible to authorized users. The Contributor Portal (Partner portal) is where data partners (like an NGO or ministry) can log in to submit data securely. It provides forms or upload tools, displays validation feedback, and allows them to see their data contributions’ status (draft, under review, published) . The Admin Portal is for YETO staff (admins, stewards, etc.) to manage the system. It includes interfaces for everything described in governance: managing the source registry (view/add/edit sources, with fields for method, cadence, license etc.), reviewing new source suggestions (with an approve/reject workflow as described), monitoring ingestion jobs (with logs of success/failure) , viewing QA dashboards (outlier flags, contradiction reports) , browsing the knowledge graph of entities (perhaps to see connections between datasets and documents), managing content (like editing a glossary term or updating a translation) , and administering user accounts/permissions. There may also be a section for managing the AI assistant knowledge base or model monitoring, given AI is part of YETO. While these portals are not exposed to end-users, the site map in documentation includes them to ensure they are built. The content coverage for these is more about functionality than data: they need to display information from the database like sources list, tickets, user roles. The expanded registry we provided is essentially what populates the Source Management page in the admin portal. For instance, an admin can see the table of sources (like we presented), filter by those needing credentials, or click one to see details and edit. The contributor portal similarly shows that organization’s submitted datasets and any automated validation outcomes (like “File X: 2 new records ingested, 1 duplicate skipped, 0 errors” etc.). The Admin/Contributor portals are the operational backbone ensuring the data on public pages remains high quality and up-to-date. In our documentation, we describe them so that stakeholders know there are mechanisms for data input and oversight beyond the public-facing pages.

Finally, any additional sections not explicitly listed (for example, a Contact page with a form, or an About page describing YETO’s mission) are also considered. The sitemap above is exhaustive based on the prompt. “Contact” was mentioned in the design system snippet  – presumably a simple page with a form to reach the YETO team (and as per requirements, it will only show the official email and no physical address). An About page might be implicitly covered by Glossary/Methodology/Transparency sections combined.

In conclusion, the site map and content coverage ensure that each major aspect of Yemen’s economy and humanitarian situation has a dedicated space on YETO. The expanded source registry underpins this by tagging each data source with the relevant pages: e.g., a source like WFP prices is tagged for Local Economy, Food Security, and Prices & Cost of Living pages; a source like IMF Article IV report is linked to Macroeconomy & Public Finance pages (for data extraction) and Research library (as a document). This relational mapping allows the site to automatically populate pages with updated data. As YETO grows, new sections or indicators can be added following the same structure, but the above covers the full initial scope required for a comprehensive Yemen Economic Tracking Observatory. Each page is not an island; cross-links are provided (for instance, the Aid Flows page might link to Poverty outcomes to show how aid correlates with poverty changes, etc.), leveraging the knowledge graph of relationships between data, documents, entities, and events that YETO maintains. All of this is documented so that content managers and developers know what should appear where, and end-users can easily navigate the wealth of information through a well-organized site.


Part 3: AI Agents, Prompts & Dynamic Functionality

Objective: In this section, we design and implement intelligent AI agents that power YETO’s dynamic and interactive features. We introduce four core agents – the Manus ingestion agent, Maher AI founder assistant, sector-specific specialist agents, and the user-facing conversational assistant – and provide detailed prompt engineering instructions for each. These prompts will ensure that all AI outputs are accurate, transparent, and context-aware, in line with YETO’s mission of evidence-backed analysis. We also outline how these agents learn continuously and improve over time. All agents share YETO’s strict policies (no hallucination, provenance tracking, bilingual output, etc.) and operate on the same evidence base . Below, we detail each agent’s role and the key instructions their prompts must include.

3.1 Manus – Ingestion & ETL Agent

Role & Responsibilities: Manus is an AI-driven ETL agent responsible for carrying out the data ingestion plan (as defined in Part 1). It automates data retrieval and processing from various sources, ensuring that YETO’s databases stay up-to-date. Manus interfaces with APIs, files, web scrapers, and manual upload channels as needed. It handles scheduling of ingestion jobs, monitors for errors, manages retries on failure, and logs all steps for auditability. The Manus agent essentially acts as the orchestrator of YETO’s data pipelines, making sure each source is ingested correctly and with full provenance tracking.

Prompt Engineering Instructions (Manus): The Manus system prompt will be written to clearly define its step-by-step duties during each ingestion cycle. It should instruct Manus to perform the following tasks in a systematic, idempotent manner (so rerunning doesn’t create duplicates ):
	•	Source Registry Scanning: Begin by reading the master data source registry (a table of all datasets and sources). Identify which sources are active and due for update (vs. those pending or not yet integrated). For each active source marked for ingestion, determine the required action and method (API call, file download, web scraping, or waiting for manual input). The prompt should emphasize using the registry’s fields to guide actions – e.g. check flags like active=true or schedule=Daily/Weekly to know what to ingest next.
	•	Credential & Key Handling: If a source entry has needs_key=true or otherwise requires credentials/API keys that are not available, Manus should skip automated retrieval for that source and alert an administrator. The prompt must make it clear that Manus never attempts unauthorized access or fabrication of credentials (per YETO’s “No Hallucination” rule ). Instead, it should log a notice that the source requires a key or manual upload, and perhaps generate a task or notification for the admin to provide that data. This ensures compliance with licensing and access constraints – Manus only ingests what it is authorized to.
	•	Data Retrieval & Execution: For each source that is ready and has no unmet credential requirements, Manus calls the appropriate ingestion routine:
	•	API sources: Construct the API request (using stored endpoints and parameters from the registry) and fetch the latest data. Handle pagination or rate limits if specified.
	•	File downloads: Fetch files from URLs or servers (e.g. .csv, .xlsx, .zip) as listed. Save raw files in the designated raw data storage (with proper filenames and timestamps).
	•	Web scraping: If a source is a webpage (e.g. an online report or HTML table), run the scraper script or extraction logic associated with that source.
	•	Manual uploads: If the source is marked for manual data entry (e.g. monthly figures that a partner will upload), then Manus should simply note that this source is pending manual input and possibly send a reminder or create a placeholder. It should not attempt any fake data.
The prompt should explicitly tell Manus to use the correct tool or script for each source, as defined in the registry (e.g., call script ingest_central_bank_api() for Central Bank API data). Each ingestion action must be executed in isolation for each source, to avoid any cross-contamination of data streams.
	•	Parallel Data Stream Separation: Manus must keep parallel datasets separate. In Yemen’s context, for example, certain indicators have two parallel versions from two authorities (IRG vs DFA). The agent’s instructions should reinforce that data from different regimes or sources should not be mistakenly merged or overwritten. Each data point must be tagged with its regime/source as per the schema (the registry includes a field for “north–south regime tag” identifying whether data pertains to the IRG or DFA authority ). Under no circumstance should Manus mix data from these parallel streams – the platform even has rules to never merge different regime data unless explicitly in a compare mode . The prompt should enforce storing each source’s data separately (e.g., separate database tables or separate key prefixes for IRG vs DFA) and ensure that any automated processing respects these boundaries.
	•	Post-Ingestion Validation: After retrieving data, Manus performs validation checks on each dataset:
	•	Check for schema consistency (do the fields and formats match expected schema?).
	•	Check for completeness (did we get all expected dates/entries?).
	•	Check for anomalies (e.g., sudden large jumps or gaps in time series that might indicate an ingestion error or a data issue).
	•	If any validation rules fail, Manus should flag it. The prompt will instruct Manus to create a Data Gap Ticket for anomalies or missing data, per YETO policy. For instance, if data for a certain month is missing or out-of-range, Manus notes this in a ticket describing what is missing, why it matters, and suggested source or resolution  . These gap tickets are added to a queue for review by analysts or admins.
	•	Provenance Logging: Manus must then update the provenance ledger (the system that tracks where every data point came from). For each dataset updated, Manus records metadata including source name, original publisher, retrieval timestamp, and licensing info. YETO’s “Every Number Has a Home” rule means the system stores source publisher, dataset name, URL, publish date, retrieval date, license terms, geographic scope, time coverage, definitions/units, etc. for every statistic . The Manus prompt should explicitly remind the agent to record all these fields. For example, after ingesting “Central Bank Exchange Rates – IRG”, Manus would log: Source: Central Bank of Yemen (Aden), dataset name, URL, published date, retrieved on X date, License (e.g. public domain or CC-BY-4.0), geographic scope (IRG-controlled areas), time coverage, units (YER per USD), and so on. This step is crucial for transparency, so that any number shown on YETO has a full citation trail. If any required metadata is missing (e.g. license unknown), Manus should mark that for manual review .
	•	Translation of Metadata: Many sources will have descriptions or metadata in either Arabic or English. Manus is instructed to translate any textual metadata into the other language (to support YETO’s bilingual operation). For example, if a dataset description is provided in English, Manus uses the platform’s translation engine to produce an Arabic version, and vice versa. The prompt will mention using the controlled bilingual glossary to ensure consistent terminology  – e.g. always translate “Gross Domestic Product” to the exact Arabic term in the glossary. Manus should store both language versions of titles, descriptions, and units for each dataset. The translation engine should enforce preferred terms from the glossary and flag any unknown terms . Importantly, Manus must never alter or overwrite the original text; it stores translations alongside the original to maintain fidelity .
	•	Triggering Downstream Updates: Once new data is ingested and validated, Manus should notify or trigger downstream processes:
	•	Refresh any affected dashboards or data caches so that the front-end visualizations incorporate the latest data.
	•	Inform the search index or knowledge graph of new entries (so that the user-facing assistant and search functions can find the new data).
	•	If certain thresholds are crossed or alerts configured (e.g. an indicator changed significantly), Manus might enqueue notifications or reports.
Essentially, the prompt should remind Manus to “fan out” updates to any system components depending on the fresh data. This keeps YETO’s public site and analytical tools in sync with the ingestion.
